Papilledema 2. Stupor/coma 3. Speech deficits + confusion 4. Eye deviation 5. Ataxia 6. Motor/sensory deficits ** Diagnosis:** CT scan, MRI with gadolinium enhancement ** Treatment :** 1. Surgical removal (total or subtotal) 2. Radiation 3. Chemotherapy 4. Metastatic â†’ Whole brain radiation 5. Increased ICP â†’ Dexamethasone 6. Hydrocephalus â†’ Shunting --- ### Intracranial Aneurysms ** Definition:** Saccular berry-shaped aneurysms in Circle of Willis â†’ Rupture â†’ SAH ** Symptoms :** 1. Worst headache of life 2. Pulsating headache (with each cardiac contraction) 3. Vasospasm with infarction 4. Hydrocephalus 5. Coma/death ** Severity: Hunt-Hess Classification ** | Grade | Clinical Features | Mortality | |-------|-------------------|-----------| | I | Asymptomatic or mild headache | 11% | | II | Moderate-severe headache, or with oculomotor palsy | 26% | | III | Confused, drowsy, or mild focal signs | 37% | | IV | Stupor (localizes pain) | 71% | | V | Coma (posturing or no motor response) | 100% | ** Diagnosis :** 1. CT scan â†’ If negative â†’ LP 2. Four-vessel cerebral angiography ** Treatment :** 1. IV antihypertensives 2. Phenytoin (seizure prophylaxis) 3. Mannitol (cerebral edema) 4. Nimodipine (decrease vasospasm) 5. Emergency external ventricular drainage 6. Craniotomy + clot evacuation (rare) 7. ** Definitive**: Microsurgical clipping + Endovascular coiling --- ### Epidural Hematoma ** Pathogenesis:** Bleeding from middle meningeal artery (temporal bone fracture) ** Clinical :** 1. Loss of consciousness 2. Recovery (honeymoon period) 3. Progressive deterioration ** Signs:** Decreased GCS ** Diagnosis:** CT scan â†’ **Lens-shaped** mass ** Treatment :** 1. Airway control 2. Emergency cranial decompression 3. Burr holes over hematoma 4. Middle meningeal artery bleeding controlled with dura fixation to bone --- ### Subdural Hematoma ** Pathogenesis:** Bleeding from bridging veins (spontaneous or traumatic) ** Risk Factors :** 1. Trauma 2. Elderly patients with brain atrophy 3. Anticoagulants ** Symptoms:** Headache, drowsiness, unilateral neurological symptoms ** Diagnosis:** CT, MRI, angiography ** Treatment :** - ** Stable**: Nonoperative (ICU observation) - ** Unstable, large lesion >10mm, midline shift >5mm**: Craniotomy or burr hole --- ### Spinal Tumors ** Types :** 1. ** Extradural**: Metastatic disease (most common) 2. ** Intradural**: Meningiomas, schwannomas, neurofibromas, ependymomas 3. ** Intramedullary**: Astrocytomas , ependymomas, cavernous malformations ** Symptoms:** Back pain or neurological deficit ** Signs:** Progressive spinal cord compression (paresthesia, hypoesthesia, weakness, dropping limb) ** Diagnosis:** MRI, CT myelogram ** Treatment :** - Anterior column (vertebral bodies, disc, ligaments): Vertebral tumor resection + bone graft + metal stabilization - Posterior column (facet joint, neural arch, ligaments): Laminectomy - Metastatic/unresectable: Palliation + Radiation therapy --- ### Spondylosis & Disc Herniation ** Anatomy:** Intervertebral disc = Nucleus pulposus + Annulus fibrosus ** Pathogenesis :** - Nucleus (80% water) dehydrates with age â†’ Disc space narrows â†’ Vertebral stresses â†’ Osteophytes â†’ Nerve root trauma â†’ Spondylosis - Herniation of nucleus pulposus into spinal canal/neural foramina : - ** Lateral**: Nerve root compression â†’ Radicular symptoms - ** Central**: Myelopathy --- ### Cervical Disc Disease ** Symptoms :** 1. Age 50+ with pain, paresthesia, weakness 2. Cervical myelopathy â†’ Weakness, numbness, paresthesia of hands/forearm 3. Cervical radiculopathy â†’ Pain radiating to arm (worsens with neck extension) ** Signs :** 1. Limited neck motion + straightening 2. Sensory/motor deficits in radicular pattern 3. Diminished biceps, brachioradialis, triceps reflexes 4. Hyperreflexia with Hoffman sign and Babinski ** Diagnosis :** 1. X-ray: Straightening + disc space narrowing + osteophytes 2. CT myelography 3. MRI ** Treatment :** 1. Medical: Cervical traction, analgesics, muscle relaxants 2. Surgery: Anterior cervical fusion + bone graft + internal fixation OR Decompression laminectomy --- ### Lumbar Disc Disease ** Symptoms :** 1. Sciatica (pain to posterior thigh) 2. Radicular pain 3. Most common: L4-5 and L5-S1 â†’ Paresthesia, numbness, weakness 4. Straight leg raise test (positive) 5. Absence/weakness of ankle or knee reflex ** Diagnosis:** MRI ** Treatment :** 1. Most patients improve without surgery 2. Laminectomy + discectomy 3. ** Urgent surgery for **: - Foot drop - Cauda equina syndrome: Urinary retention/overflow incontinence + bilateral sciatica + perineal numbness --- ### Nerve Injuries | Nerve | Mechanism of Injury | Clinical Picture | |-------|---------------------|------------------| | Radial | Humeral fractures | Wrist drop | | Ulnar | â€” | Claw hand (atrophy of all hand muscles except thenar) | | Median | Compression | Carpal tunnel syndrome (paresthesia in lateral 3 digits + thenar atrophy) | | Axillary | Anterior shoulder dislocation | Loss of sensation over deltoid | | Common peroneal | â€” | Foot drop | | Long thoracic | â€” | Winging of scapula | --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø£ÙˆØ±Ø§Ù… Ø§Ù„Ø¯Ù…Ø§Øº Ù†ÙˆØ¹Ø§Ù†: Ø¯Ø§Ø®Ù„ Ø§Ù„Ù…Ø® (Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø±Ù‡Ø§ Ø§Ù„Ø£ÙˆØ±Ø§Ù… Ø§Ù„Ø¯Ø¨Ù‚ÙŠØ© ÙˆØ§Ù„Ø§Ù†ØªØ´Ø§Ø±ÙŠ) ÙˆØ®Ø§Ø±Ø¬ Ø§Ù„Ù…Ø® (Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø±Ù‡Ø§ Ø§Ù„Ø³Ø­Ø§Ø¦ÙŠØ©). E. coli (most common) 2. Proteus, Klebsiella, Enterococcus 3. Pseudomonas (hospitalized patients) ** Risk Factors:** DM, sexual intercourse, stress incontinence, previous UTIs ** Symptoms :** - ** Cystitis**: Dysuria, frequency, urgency, suprapubic pain - ** Pyelonephritis**: Flank pain, nausea, vomiting ** Signs:** Flank or suprapubic tenderness, foul-smelling urine ** Diagnosis :** 1. Urine analysis: Pyuria, hematuria, bacteriuria 2. Urine culture: â‰¥100,000 or 10,000 or 1,000 CFU/mL 3. Cystoscopy/imaging (complicated, recurrent, suspected anomaly) ** Treatment :** - Uncomplicated: Oral antibiotics - Complicated: Hospital admission + IV antibiotics ** First-choice antibiotics:** Fluoroquinolones (levofloxacin, moxifloxacin) --- ### Stone Disease ** Etiology by Type :** | Stone Type | Composition | Associated Conditions | |------------|-------------|----------------------| | ** Calcium phosphate/oxalate** | Most common | Hyperparathyroidism, hyperuricosuria | | ** Struvite** | Magnesium ammonium phosphate | Proteus (urease-producing organisms) | | ** Uric acid** | â€” | Gout, tumor, Lesch-Nyhan syndrome | | ** Cystine** | â€” | Cystinuria | ** Risk Factors :** 1. Low fluid intake 2. Low calcium diet (â†’ â†‘ oxalate excretion) 3. High sodium diet 4. High protein diet ** Symptoms :** 1. Renal colic: Flank pain radiating to groin 2. Vomiting, nausea 3. Dysuria, hematuria ** Diagnosis :** 1. Urine analysis: Hematuria, crystals 2. Abdominal X-ray: Radiopaque stones (calcium, struvite, cystine); NOT uric acid 3. U/S: Size, hydronephrosis 4. Blood tests: Electrolytes, PTH, uric acid 5. Spiral CT scan 6. Retrograde or IV pyelography ** Stone Appearance :** - Calcium oxalate: Dumbbell-shaped, bipyramidal, birefringent - Uric acid: Small, red-orange - Cystine: Flat hexagonal, yellow - Struvite: Rectangular prisms ** Treatment (by size) :** - <5 mm: Pass spontaneously + Deflazacort + Nifedipine + Tamsulosin - >5 mm: ESWL or Percutaneous nephrolithotomy - If above fails: Open pyelolithotomy ** Prevention :** - Increase fluid intake + dietary modification - Pharmacologic : - Thiazide diuretics â†’ Ca stones - Potassium citrate â†’ Cystine/uric stones - Calcium diet â†’ Oxalate stones --- ### Renal Cancer ** Pathology :** 1. Clear cell carcinoma (most common) 2. Papillary carcinoma 3. Chromophobe carcinoma 4. Rare: Collecting duct, Wilms tumor, sarcoma ** Symptoms:** Hematuria + Flank pain (if hemorrhage) + Fever (if metastasis) ** Sign:** Palpable abdominal mass ** Diagnosis :** 1. IV pyelography 2. CT scan ** Treatment :** 1. Initial: Radical nephrectomy 2. Metastatic: Chemotherapy (gemcitabine + fluorouracil) + Interleukin-2 --- ### Bladder Cancer ** Pathology :** 1. Transitional cell carcinoma (most common) 2. Squamous cell carcinoma 3. Adenocarcinoma ** Risk Factors:** Smoking, Î²-naphthylamine, schistosomiasis, paraminophenol ** Symptoms:** Hematuria, UTI, frequency, dysuria ** Diagnosis :** 1. Urine analysis 2. Cystoscopy + biopsy (confirmation) 3. Excretory urography ** Treatment :** - Local: Transurethral resection + Chemotherapy (doxorubicin, mitomycin, thiotepa) - Locally advanced: Radical cystectomy + Radiation + Gemcitabine + Cisplatin --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø§Ù„ÙƒÙ„Ù‰ Ø®Ù„Ù Ø§Ù„ØµÙØ§Ù‚ØŒ ÙˆØªØªØºØ°Ù‰ Ù…Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø´Ø±ÙŠØ§Ù† Ø§Ù„ÙƒÙ„ÙˆÙŠ. Ø³Ø±Ø·Ø§Ù† Ø§Ù„Ù…Ø«Ø§Ù†Ø© Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø±Ù‡Ø§ Ø§Ù„Ø§Ù†ØªÙ‚Ø§Ù„ÙŠ Ø§Ù„Ø®Ù„ÙˆÙŠØŒ ÙˆÙŠØ±ØªØ¨Ø· Ø¨Ø§Ù„ØªØ¯Ø®ÙŠÙ†. --- ## ðŸ“‹ CHAPTER 21: PROSTATE AND MALE REPRODUCTIVE ORGANS ### Benign Prostatic Hyperplasia (BPH) ** Definition:** Most common cause of urinary outlet obstruction in older males (>50) ** Pathogenesis:** â†‘ DHT (active testosterone metabolite) or â†‘ estrogen with aging â†’ Cellular hyperplasia of periurethral area (transitional zone) â†’ Urethral compression ** Symptoms :** 1. Obstructive: Urinary hesitancy, intermittency, decreased stream, incomplete emptying 2. UTI + stones 3. Bilateral hydroureteronephrosis â†’ Renal failure ** Signs :** - PR: Enlarged, symmetric, rubbery prostate - Suprapubic: Distended bladder ** Diagnosis :** 1. Urine analysis + culture 2. KFT 3. Post-void cystourethrogram (residual volume >50 mL) 4. Transrectal U/S, IV pyelography, CT ** Treatment :** 1. ** Medical **: - Alpha-blockers (terazosin) â†’ Side effect: Postural hypotension - 5- Î±-reductase inhibitors (finasteride) â†’ Less effective than alpha-blockers 2. ** Surgical (TURP or transurethral needle ablation) ** : - Indications : - >100 mL residual volume - Acute urinary retention - Chronic retention with overflow dribbling - Recurrent hematuria or UTI --- ### Prostate Cancer ** Pathogenesis:** Adenocarcinoma from peripheral zone (testosterone stimulation) â†’ Estrogen/antiandrogens inhibit ** Symptoms :** - Most commonly asymptomatic (screening detection) - Obstructive symptoms: Hesitancy, intermittency, decreased stream, incomplete emptying ** Screening :** 1. PR: Enlarged, asymmetrical, nodular prostate 2. PSA ** Diagnosis (after nodule on PR) :** 1. Transrectal U/S with needle biopsy 2. Metastasis: Chest X-ray, LFT, bone scan ** Treatment :** - ** Local **: - Radical prostatectomy - External beam radiotherapy - Interstitial irradiation (implants) - ** Local spread**: Radiation + Hormonal therapy - ** Metastatic**: Hormonal ablation - Bilateral surgical orchiectomy - Chemical: LHRH antagonist + Antiandrogens (flutamide, cyproterone) --- Part 1: General Surgical Principles & Techniques 1. Surgical Instruments & Sutures Detailed Explanation: Surgical blades are numbered by size and purpose: Blades 10, 20, and 22 are used for large abdominal or thoracic skin incisions, while Blade 11 is specialized for draining abscesses or incising blood vessels. Scissors are task-specific: Metzenbaum are general-purpose, Iris are for delicate dissection, and Mayo scissors are reserved for thick tissues like fascia or tendons. Suture Materials: Sutures are classified as Absorbable (Plain Gut, Vicryl , Monocryl, PDS) or Non-Absorbable ( Prolene , Nylon, Silk). Vicryl : Synthetic polyfilament ; absorbs in ~3 months; used for dermis and muscle. PDS (Polydioxanone): Synthetic monofilament; maintains strength for 8 weeks; ideal for fascia. Nylon/ Prolene : Non-absorbable; used for skin and vascular anastomoses. High-Yield Exam Points: Lidocaine (Xylocaine): Immediate onset; duration 60-120 min; max dose 4.5 mg/kg. Bupivacaine (Marcaine): Slower onset; duration 240-480 min; max dose 3 mg/kg. Antibiotic Prophylaxis: Must be given 1 hour before incision and redosed after 4 hours for long procedures. Pre-operative & Post-operative Care Clinical Presentation & Management: Pre-op Preparation (Ascites): Patients with severe ascites require Fresh Frozen Plasma (FFP) to decrease bleeding tendency and portal pressure before surgery. Post-operative Fever (The 5 Wâ€™s): Wind (POD 1-2): Atelectasis/Pneumonia. Water (POD 3-5): UTI. Walking (POD 5-7): DVT/Thrombophlebitis. Wound (POD 7): Wound infection. Wonder (POD 8-15): Drug fever or deep abscess. Massive Transfusion: Defined as 2500 mL at once or 5000 mL over 24 hours. Complications: Hyperkalemia, hypocalcemia (citrate toxicity), acidosis, and hypothermia. Ù†Ù‚Ù„ Ø§Ù„Ø¯Ù… Ø¨ÙƒÙ…ÙŠØ§Øª ÙƒØ¨ÙŠØ±Ø© Ù‚Ø¯ ÙŠØ³Ø¨Ø¨ Ø§Ø±ØªÙØ§Ø¹ Ø§Ù„Ø¨ÙˆØªØ§Ø³ÙŠÙˆÙ… ÙˆÙ†Ù‚Øµ Ø§Ù„ÙƒØ§Ù„Ø³ÙŠÙˆÙ… ÙˆÙ‡Ø¨ÙˆØ· Ø­Ø±Ø§Ø±Ø© Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ø³Ù… [277 ØŒ 1237 -1243]. 3. Wound Healing Core Topics: Primary Intention: Clean wounds closed by sutures; minimal scar. Secondary Intention: Gaped/infected wounds left to heal by granulation; larger "ugly" scar. Cellular Sequence: Platelets (hours) â†’ PMNs (0-2 days) â†’ Macrophages (3-4 days, the "main cell") â†’ Fibroblasts (5 days). Exam Trap: Keloid vs. Hypertrophic Scar. Keloid: Extends beyond the original wound margins; more common in dark-skinned individuals; does not regress. Hypertrophic: Stays within the wound margins; usually regresses over time. Keratin layer (dead skin) 2. Granular layer 3. Squamous layer 4. Basal layer (attached to basement membrane) ** Dermis:** Collagen, elastin, glycosaminoglycans, fibroblasts ** Subcutaneous fat:** Sweat glands, hair follicles, sebaceous glands --- ### Basal Cell Carcinoma (BCC) - ** Most common skin cancer ** - Most commonly on sun-exposed areas - Arises from pluripotent epithelial cells ** Types :** | Type | Description | Treatment | |------|-------------|-----------| | ** Noduloulcerative ** (most common) | Pearly, dome-shaped nodular with telangiectasia and ulcerated center | <1 cm: Cautery/curettage/cryosurgery; >1 cm: Surgical excision (Mohs) | | ** Superficial** | Thin, scaly pink plaques, irregular margins, horizontally expanding | Wide margin excision + skin graft; Topical chemo (fluorouracil) + cryosurgery | | ** Sclerosing** | Smooth, atrophic, indurated (mimics scar tissue) | Mohs micrographic surgery | --- ### Melanoma ** Definition:** Malignant transformation of melanocytes in basal layer of epidermis ** Pathogenesis:** UV light â†’ Malignant transformation ** Risk Factors :** 1. Mole change (shape, size, color) 2. >100 nevi 3. Personal/family history of melanoma 4. Excess sun exposure + tendency to freckle/sunburn ** ABCDE Signs :** - ** A**symmetry - ** B**order irregular - ** C** olor mottled - ** D** iameter large - ** E** nlargement ** Types :** 1. ** Superficial spreading** (most common): Heterogeneous pigmentation, irregular margins (age 40-50) 2. ** Lentigo maligna **: Sun-exposed areas (malar, cheek, temple) (age 70) 3. ** Acral lentiginous**: Palms, soles, nail beds, mucous membranes 4. ** Nodular melanoma ** ** Treatment :** 1. Main: Surgical excision 2. LN metastases: Sentinel lymph node biopsy 3. Metastatic: Surgery + Radiation + Chemotherapy --- ### Squamous Cell Carcinoma (SCC) ** Pathogenesis :** 1. Sunlight exposure 2. HPV 3. Immunosuppression 4. Chronic ulcers 5. Ionizing radiation 6. Tobacco use (lip, gum, tongue) 7. Scars (burns â†’ Marjolin's ulcer) ** Symptoms:** Nodule or plaque with ulceration on sun-exposed area ** Signs :** 1. Pinkish or hypo/hyperpigmented lesion 2. Regional lymphadenopathy 3. Cutaneous horns ** Treatment :** 1. Surgical excision Â± grafting 2. Small tumors: Cautery/curettage --- ### Kaposi Sarcoma ** Etiology:** Human herpesvirus 8 (HHV-8) ** Clinical:** Purplish lesions on skin, predominantly with HIV and CD4 <100/mmÂ³ ** Treatment :** 1. Antiretroviral therapy (raise CD4 count) 2. Chemotherapy: Liposomal doxorubicin or vinblastine --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø³Ø±Ø·Ø§Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù„Ø¯ Ø«Ù„Ø§Ø«Ø© Ø£Ù†ÙˆØ§Ø¹ Ø±Ø¦ÙŠØ³ÙŠØ©: Ø³Ø±Ø·Ø§Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø®Ù„Ø§ÙŠØ§ Ø§Ù„Ù‚Ø§Ø¹Ø¯ÙŠØ© (Ø§Ù„Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø± Ø´ÙŠÙˆØ¹Ø§Ù‹ØŒ ÙŠØ¸Ù‡Ø± ÙƒØ¹Ù‚ÙŠØ¯Ø© Ù„Ø¤Ù„Ø¤ÙŠØ© Ù…Ø¹ ØªÙ‚Ø±Ø­ØŒ Ù†Ø§Ø¯Ø±Ø§Ù‹ Ù…Ø§ ÙŠÙ†ØªØ´Ø±)ØŒ Ø³Ø±Ø·Ø§Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø®Ù„Ø§ÙŠØ§ Ø§Ù„Ø­Ø±Ø´ÙÙŠØ© (Ù…Ø±ØªØ¨Ø· Ø¨Ø§Ù„Ø´Ù…Ø³ØŒ HPV ØŒ Ø§Ù„ØªØ«Ø¨ÙŠØ· Ø§Ù„Ù…Ù†Ø§Ø¹ÙŠØŒ Ù‚Ø¯ ÙŠÙ†ØªØ´Ø±)ØŒ ÙˆØ§Ù„ÙˆØ±Ù… Ø§Ù„Ù…ÙŠÙ„Ø§Ù†ÙŠÙ†ÙŠ (Ø§Ù„Ø£Ø®Ø·Ø±ØŒ ÙŠØªØ¨Ø¹ Ù‚Ø§Ø¹Ø¯Ø© ABCDE ØŒ ÙˆÙŠØ´Ø®Øµ Ø¨Ø§Ù„Ø®Ø²Ø¹Ø©). Ø¹Ù„Ø§Ø¬ Ø³Ø±Ø·Ø§Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø®Ù„Ø§ÙŠØ§ Ø§Ù„Ù‚Ø§Ø¹Ø¯ÙŠØ© ÙˆØ§Ù„Ø­Ø±Ø´ÙÙŠØ© Ø¬Ø±Ø§Ø­ÙŠØŒ ÙˆØ§Ù„Ù…ÙŠÙ„Ø§Ù†ÙŠÙ†ÙŠ ÙŠØ­ØªØ§Ø¬ Ø§Ø³ØªØ¦ØµØ§Ù„ ÙˆØ§Ø³Ø¹ ÙˆÙ‚Ø¯ ÙŠØ­ØªØ§Ø¬ ÙƒÙŠÙ…Ø§ÙˆÙŠ Ø£Ùˆ Ø¥Ø´Ø¹Ø§Ø¹ÙŠ. --- ## ðŸ“‹ CHAPTER 23: SKIN INFECTION AND HYPERSENSITIVITY ### Bullous/Blistering Diseases ** Pemphigus Vulgaris :** - Autoimmune: Antibodies attack epidermal cell adhesion ( desmogleins ) - Age 30-40 - ** Clinical**: Oral erosions first, thin/flaccid bullae (burn-like), Nikolsky sign (+), painful (not pruritic) - ** Diagnosis**: Skin biopsy + Immunofluorescence â†’ IgG + C3 in epidermis - ** Treatment**: Systemic glucocorticoids (prednisone) â†’ If fails: Azathioprine, cyclophosphamide, mycophenolate, rituximab, IVIG ** Bullous Pemphigoid :** - More common than vulgaris, age 70-80 - Thicker bullae (defect at dermo-epidermal junction), oral lesions rare - Less infection, less fluid loss â†’ Better prognosis - ** Diagnosis**: Immunofluorescence â†’ Antibodies at dermo-epidermal junction - ** Treatment**: Prednisone (gold standard); Tetracycline/erythromycin + nicotinamide alternative; Topical steroids for oral lesions ** Porphyria Cutanea Tarda :** - Deficiency of uroporphyrinogen decarboxylase â†’ â†‘ Porphyrins â†’ Photosensitivity - ** Clinical**: Liver disease (HIV, alcohol, OCP), fragile nonhealing blisters on sun-exposed areas, hyperpigmentation, hypertrichosis - ** Diagnosis**: Urinary uroporphyrins > coproporphyrins - ** Treatment**: Stop alcohol, discontinue estrogen, sun protection, phlebotomy, deferoxamine, chloroquine --- ### Hypersensitivity ** Urticaria (Hives) :** - Localized hypersensitivity ( IgE + mast cells) - ** Causes**: Drugs (aspirin, NSAIDs, morphine, penicillins , ACE inhibitors), Foods (peanuts, shellfish, tomatoes), Latex - ** Clinical**: Acute wheals/hives within 30 min (<24 hours); Chronic >6 weeks; Dermatographism - ** Treatment**: H1 antihistamines; Severe: H2 antihistamines + systemic steroids; Chronic: Newer nonsedating antihistamines ** Morbilliform Rashes :** - Drug reaction (lymphocyte-mediated), milder than urticaria - Generalized maculopapular eruption, blanches with pressure - ** Treatment**: Antihistamines + Steroids ** Erythema Multiforme :** - Most common cause: Infection (herpes simplex, mycoplasma) - Target-like lesions on palms/soles, no mucous membrane involvement - ** Treatment**: Antihistamines + Treat underlying infection ** Stevens-Johnson Syndrome (Erythema Multiforme Major) :** - Hypersensitivity to drugs (penicillin, sulfa, NSAIDs, phenytoin, phenobarbital) - <10-15% TBSA, mucous membrane involvement (oral, conjunctivae), respiratory tract involvement - ** Treatment**: Burn unit admission, withdrawal of offending drug, supportive care, mechanical ventilation if needed, IVIG ** Toxic Epidermal Necrolysis (TEN) :** - Most severe version, 30-100% TBSA, Nikolsky sign (+) - ** Treatment**: Same as Stevens-Johnson ** Fixed Drug Reaction :** - Localized reaction on same site with repeated drug exposure (aspirin, NSAIDs, tetracycline, barbiturates) - Round, sharply demarcated lesions leave hyperpigmented spot - ** Treatment**: Drug discontinuation, topical steroids ** Erythema Nodosum :** - Inflammatory panniculitis - ** Causes**: Streptococcal infection, coccidioidomycosis, histoplasmosis, sarcoidosis, IBD, TB, hepatitis, Yersinia - Multiple painful red raised nodules on anterior lower limb (don't ulcerate, last ~6 weeks) - ** Treatment**: Self-limiting; Treat underlying disease; Analgesics/NSAIDs; Potassium iodide --- ### Fungal Infections ** Types:** Tinea pedis, cruris, corporis, versicolor, capitis, onychomycosis ** Diagnosis:** Visual appearance, KOH test (fungal hyphae), culture (most accurate) ** Treatment :** - Onychomycosis/tinea capitis: Oral terbinafine or itraconazole - Skin (no hair/nails): Topical: ketoconazole, clotrimazole, econazole, terbinafine, miconazole ** Tinea Versicolor :** - Etiology: Pityrosporum orbiculare (Malassezia furfur) - Clinical: Tan/brown/white scaling macules (chest, neck, abdomen, face), lesions do not tan - ** Diagnosis**: KOH â†’ "Spaghetti and meatballs " - ** Treatment**: Topical selenium sulfide, clotrimazole, ketoconazole, oral itraconazole - * Distinguish from vitiligo: Vitiligo has no pigmentation; Tinea versicolor has altered pigmentation * ** Candidiasis (Candida albicans) :** - ** Risk factors**: Systemic antibiotics, obesity, HIV, DM, pregnancy, corticosteroids, debilitating disease - ** Clinical **: - Intertriginous: Well-demarcated erythematous patches with pustules (groin, axilla, inframammary) - Vulvovaginitis: White/yellow discharge - Oral thrush: White patches on tongue/buccal mucosa - Candidal paronychia: Painful red swelling around nail - ** Diagnosis**: KOH, culture - ** Treatment**: Topical nystatin, clotrimazole, miconazole; Systemic amphotericin (serious infections) --- ### Bacterial Infections ** Impetigo :** - Superficial bacterial infection (epidermis only) - ** Etiology**: Group A Î²-hemolytic streptococcus OR S. aureus (bullous impetigo) - ** Clinical**: Maculopapules â†’ Vesicular pustules/bullae â†’ Golden/yellow crusts ("honey-colored") - Can progress to lymphangitis, furunculosis, cellulitis, acute glomerulonephritis - ** Treatment**: Oral 1st-gen cephalosporin or semisynthetic penicillin; Topical mupirocin/bacitracin; Penicillin-allergic: Macrolides; MRSA: TMP/SMX, clindamycin, doxycycline ** Erysipelas :** - Etiology: Group A streptococcus (deeper than impetigo â†’ dermis + epidermis) - Clinical: Bright red, swollen, shiny, indurated, tender lesions (face), fever, chills, bacteremia - ** Treatment**: Semisynthetic penicillin, 1st-gen cephalosporin ** Cellulitis :** - Etiology: Staphylococcus, Streptococcus (dermis + subcutaneous) - Clinical: Redness, swelling, warmth, tenderness - ** Treatment**: Oxacillin, nafcillin, cefazolin; MRSA: Clindamycin, TMP/SMX, doxycycline ** Folliculitis, Furuncles, Carbuncles :** - 3 degrees of staphylococcal infections around hair follicle - ** Folliculitis**: Superficial, non-tender - ** Furuncle**: Small collection of infected material, tender - ** Carbuncle**: Confluent furuncles, tender - ** Treatment**: Warm compresses (folliculitis); Systemic antistaphylococcal antibiotics (furuncles); IV dicloxacillin, cephalexin (carbuncles) ** Necrotizing Fasciitis :** - ** Etiology**: Streptococcus, Clostridium (toxins damage fascia) - ** Clinical**: Very high fever, portal of entry, pain out of proportion to appearance, bullae, crepitus - ** Diagnosis**: High CPK, X-ray/CT/MRI (air in tissue), surgical debridement (confirmation) - ** Treatment**: Surgery (mainstay); Antibiotics: Î²-lactam/Î²-lactamase (ampicillin/sulbactam, ticarcillin/clavulanate, piperacillin/tazobactam) ** Paronychia :** - Infection under skin surrounding nail - ** Treatment**: Small incision + drainage + Antistaphylococcal antibiotics (dicloxacillin, cephalexin, oxacillin, nafcillin) --- ### Viral Infections ** Herpes Simplex :** - Clinical: Multiple, painful vesicles - ** Diagnosis**: Tzanck smear (multinucleated giant cells - initial), Viral culture (most accurate, 24-48 hours) - ** Treatment**: Oral acyclovir, famciclovir, valacyclovir; Acyclovir-resistant: Foscarnet ** Herpes Zoster/Varicella (Chickenpox/Shingles) :** - Varicella: Primary disease in children; Complications: Pneumonia, hepatitis, dissemination - Herpes zoster: Dermatomal ("shingles") - Clinical: Vesicles 2-3 mm at all stages on erythematous base - ** Diagnosis**: Tzanck prep (initial), Viral culture (accurate) - ** Treatment**: Chickenpox: Generally not treated; Acyclovir/valacyclovir/famciclovir for immunocompromised or adult primary infection; Gabapentin/TCAs/topical capsaicin for pain; Gabapentin for postherpetic neuralgia ** HHV Types :** - HHV-1: HSV-1 (orolabial) - HHV-2: HSV-2 (genital) - HHV-3: Varicella-zoster (chickenpox/shingles) - HHV-4: EBV (infectious mononucleosis, Burkitt lymphoma) - HHV-5: CMV - HHV-6: Roseola infantum - HHV-7: Roseola infantum - HHV-8: Kaposi sarcoma ** Molluscum Contagiosum :** - Etiology: Poxvirus (skin-to-skin or sexual) - Clinical: Asymptomatic, central umbilicated papules anywhere - ** Diagnosis**: Appearance, Giemsa stain (large cells with inclusion bodies) - ** Treatment**: Freezing, curettage, electrocautery, cantharidin --- ### Parasitic Infections ** Scabies :** - Etiology: Sarcoptes scabiei (skin-to-skin transmission) - Clinical: Web spaces of hands/feet, pruritic lesions around penis, breasts, axillary folds; Norwegian scabies in immunocompromised - ** Diagnosis**: Scraping out organism after mineral oil - ** Treatment**: Permethrin; Lindane (NOT in pregnancy); Ivermectin ** Pediculosis (Lice) :** - ** Types **: - Head: Pediculus humanus capitis - Body: Pediculus humanus corporis - Pubic: Phthirus pubis (crab louse) - Clinical: Itching, excoriations, erythematous macules/papules, secondary infection - ** Diagnosis**: Direct examination of area, axillae, scalp - ** Treatment**: Permethrin, Lindane --- ### Toxin-Mediated Diseases ** Toxic Shock Syndrome :** - Etiology: Toxin from Staphylococcus attached to foreign body (tampons, nasal packing, retained sutures) - ** Clinical **: 1. Fever >102Â°F 2. Systolic BP <90 mmHg 3. Desquamative rash 4. Vomiting 5. Mucous membrane involvement 6. Elevated bilirubin 7. Platelets <100,000 (CNS dysfunction) 8. High creatinine, CPK, LFTs 9. Hypocalcemia - ** Treatment**: Remove source, fluid resuscitation, pressors, Antibiotics: Clindamycin + (vancomycin/oxacillin) ** Staphylococcal Scalded Skin Syndrome (SSSS) :** - Etiology: Staphylococcus toxin (physical contact) - Clinical: Loss of superficial epidermis in sheets, Nikolsky sign (+), Normal BP, NO liver/kidney/bone marrow/CNS involvement - ** Treatment**: Burn unit admission, Antistaphylococcal antibiotics (oxacillin, nafcillin); Vancomycin for MRSA --- ### Benign & Precancerous Lesions ** Seborrheic Keratosis :** - Benign hyperpigmented lesions in elderly - " Stuck-on" appearance on face, shoulders, chest, back - ** Treatment**: Cosmetic removal with liquid nitrogen or curettage ** Actinic Keratosis :** - Precancerous lesions on sun-exposed areas - Usually asymptomatic, can be tender, lighter in color - ** Treatment**: Sunscreen, Cryotherapy, Topical 5-FU, Imiquimod, Topical retinoic acid derivatives, Curettage --- ### Scaling Disorders (Eczema)/ Papulosquamous Dermatitis ** Psoriasis :** - Unknown etiology - Clinical: Silvery scales on extensor surfaces, nail pitting, Koebner phenomenon (lesions develop with epidermal injury) - ** Treatment**: Salicylic acid (remove collections), Topical steroids, Topical vitamin D (calcipotriene) + Vitamin A derivatives (tazarotene), Emollients (Eucerin), UV light (>30% body surface), Methotrexate (severe), Biologics (alefacept, efalizumab, etanercept, infliximab) ** Atopic Dermatitis :** - Extraordinarily pruritic disorder, high IgE - Clinical: Red, itchy plaques on flexor surfaces; Children: Cheeks/scalp; Adults: Lichenification - ** Treatment**: Emollients, Avoid hot water/drying soaps, Cotton clothes, Topical antihistamines/coal tars/phototherapy, Antistaphylococcal antibiotics ( impetigination ), Topical immunosuppressants (tacrolimus, pimecrolimus ), Topical doxepin (pruritus) ** Seborrheic Dermatitis :** - Etiology: Oversecretion of sebum + hypersensitivity to Pityrosporum ovale - Clinical: Dandruff, scaly/greasy/flaky skin on red base (scalp, eyebrows, nasolabial fold) - ** Treatment**: Low-potency topical steroids, Topical antifungals (ketoconazole, sulfide shampoos), Zinc pyrithione shampoo ** Stasis Dermatitis :** - Etiology: Hyperpigmentation from hemosiderin in tissue, venous incompetence - ** Treatment**: No way to reverse; Elevation of legs + Support hose ** Contact Dermatitis :** - Hypersensitivity to soaps, detergents, latex, sunscreens, neomycin, jewelry - Clinical: Linear, streaked vesicles (especially poison ivy) - ** Diagnosis**: Patch testing - ** Treatment**: Antihistamines + Topical steroids ** Pityriasis Rosea :** - Pruritic eruption beginning with "herald patch " - Clinical: Erythematous, salmon-colored lesions (looks like secondary syphilis but spares palms/soles, has herald patch); Christmas tree pattern on back - ** Diagnosis**: Clinical; VDRL/RPR negative - ** Treatment**: Topical steroids --- ### Ulcers ** Causes of Ulcers :** 1. Venous disease (lower limb) 2. Diabetic foot (sole) 3. Rheumatoid disease 4. Malignant ( Marjolin's ulcer) 5. Autoimmune (SLE, systemic sclerosis) 6. Sickle cell anemia, congenital spherocytosis 7. Peripheral arterial disease (distal limb â†’ tips of toes) 8. Neuropathic ulcer 9. Trauma (pressure) 10. Infection (TB, syphilis) ** Pressure (Decubitus) Ulcers (Bedsores) :** - Immobilized/bedridden patients - Pressure areas where bone is close to skin - ** Stages **: - **1**: Nonblanchable redness - **2**: Superficial epidermis destruction or partial dermis destruction - **3**: Full-thickness skin destruction - **4**: Destruction all the way to bone - ** Diagnosis**: NEVER culture superficial swab; Definitive diagnosis in OR after debridement - ** Treatment**: Relieve pressure, Antibiotics if infected ** Venous Ulcer :** - ** Etiology**: DVT (most common), Primary varicose veins - Pathogenesis: Venous hypertension â†’ Capillary loops â†’ Leukocyte sequestration â†’ Proteolytic enzymes â†’ Endothelial injury â†’ Fibrosis + Inflammation â†’ Ulcer - ** Clinical**: Single punched-out (early) or sloping (late) ulcer with hyperpigmented margins above medial malleolus - ** Diagnosis**: Doppler, duplex, Biopsy - ** Treatment **: - Conservative: Leg elevation, Elastic stockings, Moist saline dressing - Surgical: Cockett's operation (subfascial ligation of ankle perforators); Skin graft --- ### Hair ** Alopecia Areata :** - Autoimmune: Antibodies attack hair follicles - ** Treatment**: Resolves spontaneously; Localized steroid injection ** Telogen Effluvium :** - Hair loss in response to overwhelming physiologic stress (cancer, malnutrition) - ** Treatment**: Correct underlying stress/disease --- ### Acne ** Etiology:** Propionibacterium acnes ** Clinical :** - Closed comedones (whiteheads) - Open comedones (blackheads) ** Treatment :** - ** Mild**: Topical antibiotics (clindamycin, erythromycin, sulfacetamide) + Benzoyl peroxide - ** Moderate**: Benzoyl peroxide + Retinoids (tazarotene, tretinoin) + Adapalene - ** Severe cystic**: Oral antibiotics (minocycline, tetracycline, clindamycin) + Oral isotretinoin (strong teratogen) --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø§Ù„Ø£Ù…Ø±Ø§Ø¶ Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù„Ø¯ÙŠØ© Ø§Ù„Ù…Ù†Ø§Ø¹ÙŠØ©: Ø§Ù„ÙÙ‚Ø§Ø¹ Ø§Ù„Ø´Ø§Ø¦Ø¹ (Ø£Ø¬Ø³Ø§Ù… Ù…Ø¶Ø§Ø¯Ø© Ù„Ù„Ø¯ÙŠØ³Ù…ÙˆØ¬Ù„ÙŠÙ†ØŒ ÙÙ‚Ø§Ø¹Ø§Øª Ø±Ù‚ÙŠÙ‚Ø©ØŒ Ø¹Ù„Ø§Ù…Ø© Ù†ÙŠÙƒÙˆÙ„Ø³ÙƒÙŠ Ù…ÙˆØ¬Ø¨Ø©)ØŒ ÙˆØ§Ù„ÙÙ‚Ø§Ø¹ Ø§Ù„ÙÙ‚Ø§Ø¹ÙŠ (Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø± Ø´ÙŠÙˆØ¹Ø§Ù‹ Ø¹Ù†Ø¯ ÙƒØ¨Ø§Ø± Ø§Ù„Ø³Ù†ØŒ ÙÙ‚Ø§Ø¹Ø§Øª Ø³Ù…ÙŠÙƒØ©ØŒ ØªØ´Ø®ÙŠØµ Ø¨Ø§Ù„Ù…Ù†Ø§Ø¹Ø© è§å…‰ ÙŠØ© ). Ø§Ù„Ø§Ù„ØªÙ‡Ø§Ø¨Ø§Øª Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù„Ø¯ÙŠØ©: Ø§Ù„Ù‚ÙˆØ¨Ø§Ø¡ (Ø·Ø¨Ù‚Ø© Ø³Ø·Ø­ÙŠØ©ØŒ Ù‚Ø´ÙˆØ± Ø¹Ø³Ù„ÙŠØ©)ØŒ Ø§Ù„Ø­Ù…Ø±Ø© (Ø¹Ù…ÙŠÙ‚Ø©ØŒ ÙˆØ¬Ù‡)ØŒ Ø§Ù„ØªÙ‡Ø§Ø¨ Ø§Ù„Ù†Ø³ÙŠØ¬ Ø§Ù„Ø®Ù„ÙˆÙŠ (Ø£Ø¹Ù…Ù‚ØŒ Ø§Ø­Ù…Ø±Ø§Ø± ÙˆØªÙˆØ±Ù…)ØŒ ÙˆØ§Ù„ØªÙ‡Ø§Ø¨ Ø§Ù„Ù„ÙØ§ÙØ© Ø§Ù„Ù†Ø§Ø®Ø± (Ø·Ø§Ø±Ø¦ØŒ Ø£Ù„Ù… Ø®Ø§Ø±Ø¬ Ø§Ù„ØªÙ†Ø§Ø³Ø¨ØŒ ØºØ§Ø²Ø§Øª ÙÙŠ Ø§Ù„Ù†Ø³ÙŠØ¬). Ø§Ù„Ø§Ù„ØªÙ‡Ø§Ø¨Ø§Øª Ø§Ù„ÙÙŠØ±ÙˆØ³ÙŠØ©: Ø§Ù„Ù‡Ø±Ø¨Ø³ Ø§Ù„Ø¨Ø³ÙŠØ· (Ø­ÙˆÙŠØµÙ„Ø§Øª Ù…Ø¤Ù„Ù…Ø©)ØŒ ÙˆØ§Ù„Ø­Ù…Ø§Ù‚ Ø§Ù„Ù†Ø·Ø§Ù‚ÙŠ (Ø¬Ø¯Ø±ÙŠ Ø§Ù„Ù…Ø§Ø¡/Ø§Ù„Ø­Ø²Ø§Ù… Ø§Ù„Ù†Ø§Ø±ÙŠ). Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ø±Ø¨ ÙˆØ§Ù„Ù‚Ù…Ù„ Ù…Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø·ÙÙŠÙ„ÙŠØ§Øª. Ù…ØªÙ„Ø§Ø²Ù…Ø© Ø§Ù„ØµØ¯Ù…Ø© Ø§Ù„ØªØ³Ù…Ù…ÙŠ Ø© ÙˆØ¥ØµØ§Ø¨Ø© Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù„Ø¯ Ø¨Ø§Ù„Ù…ÙƒÙˆØ±Ø§Øª Ø§Ù„Ø¹Ù†Ù‚ÙˆØ¯ÙŠØ© Ù…Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø£Ù…Ø±Ø§Ø¶ Ø§Ù„Ù†Ø§Ø¬Ù…Ø© Ø¹Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø³Ù…ÙˆÙ…. --- ## ðŸ“‹ CHAPTER 24: ORTHOPEDIC SURGERY ### Osteoarthritis - ** Most common joint disease ** - Loss of articular cartilage in synovial joints (repetitive load-bearing stress, intrinsic properties, loss of repair ability) ** Most Common Joints :** 1. 1 st carpometacarpal joint 2. Hip 3. Knee 4. Spine ** Risk Factors:** Age, obesity, female sex - ** Secondary OA**: Trauma, inflammatory joint disease, hemochromatosis, Wilson's, acromegaly, DM ** Symptoms :** - >55 years old - Deep ache increased by activity - Stiffness at rest - Limitation of movements - Tenderness, crepitus - No systemic symptoms ** Specific :** - Hip OA: Groin pain, painful internal rotation with restriction - Knee OA: Stiff knee, pain with prolonged sitting, walking, climbing stairs - 1 st CMC OA: Stiffness + pain; Grind test positive ** Diagnosis:** X-ray: Subchondral cysts, bone sclerosis, osteophytes; Synovial fluid: Leukocytosis ** Treatment :** - ** Medical**: Muscle exercises, NSAIDs (acetaminophen), Glucosamine + chondroitin sulfate, Intra-articular corticosteroids, Hyaluronic acid injection - ** Surgical**: Osteotomy, Total joint arthroplasty, Hemiarthroplasty * Note: Any hip/knee replacement â†’ Anticoagulants (warfarin INR 2-3, aspirin, heparin, LMWH, or IVC filter) * --- ### Knee Pain ** Meniscal Injury :** - Most common: Medial meniscus - ** Symptoms**: Pain, knee locking/clicking, effusions - ** Diagnosis**: McMurray test, Grinding and distraction ( Apley test), CT (most accurate) - ** Treatment **: - Conservative if tear <10 mm or partial thickness or radial tear <3 mm - Arthroscopic meniscectomy/meniscus repair for locking ** Cruciate Ligament Injury :** - Most common: ACL tear (twist or hyperextension trauma) - Pain improves initially but chronic pain develops - ** Diagnosis**: Lachman test - ** Treatment**: Conservative (strengthen surrounding muscles) OR Arthroscopic allograft/autograft ** Patellofemoral Syndrome :** - Patella misalignment and abnormal tracking - ** Symptoms**: "Theater sign" (pain sitting long period), Pain exacerbated by running/stairs, Tenderness under patella - ** Diagnosis**: Increased Q-angle - ** Treatment**: Exercises strengthening quadriceps, Stretching iliotibial band, Patellar taping/ bracing --- ### Shoulder Pain ** Impingement Syndrome :** - Rotator cuff tendonitis + Subacromial bursitis - ** Diagnosis**: Neer and Hawkins tests â†’ Pain improves after lidocaine injection - ** Treatment**: Physical therapy, Rotator cuff strengthening exercises, Subacromial corticosteroid injection ** Biceps Tendonitis :** - Inflammation of biceps tendon in bicipital groove - ** Diagnosis**: Speed test ** Glenohumeral Labral Tear :** - ** Diagnosis**: O'Brien test (pain with internal rotation, not external) OR MRI (best) - ** Treatment**: Physical therapy / Intra-articular corticosteroid injection ** Rotator Cuff Tears :** - Rotator cuff: Supraspinatus, infraspinatus, subscapularis, teres minor - ** Supraspinatus most common to tear ** - Result: Weak abduction from 0-30Â° - ** Diagnosis**: MRI --- ### Back Pain ** Facet Joint Pain :** - Synovial joint; Common source of back pain - Pain increases with trunk extension and oblique extension - ** Treatment**: Physical therapy, Facet joint injection, Radiofrequency neurotomy ** Neoplasm :** - Cancer pain worse at night, wakes patient from sleep - ** Diagnosis**: X-ray, Bone scan, MRI ** Infection (Osteomyelitis) :** - Most common in debilitated patients and IV drug abusers - Pain constant, ESR high - ** Diagnosis**: X-ray, MRI --- ### Hip Fracture ** Types :** 1. Femoral neck fracture 2. Intertrochanteric fracture 3. Subtrochanteric fracture ** Symptoms:** Older adult falls â†’ Hip, groin, knee pain; Weight bearing difficult ** Signs :** - Femoral neck: Shortened + Externally rotated extremity - Hip motion painful ** Diagnosis:** X-ray, MRI ** Treatment :** - Acetabulum involved â†’ Total hip replacement - Intertrochanteric â†’ ORIF (sliding screw hip) * Note: Hip fracture patients should receive anticoagulants * --- ### Compartment Syndrome ** Definition:** Increased pressure within osteofascial compartment â†’ Ischemic necrosis ** Etiology :** 1. Fractures 2. Vascular compromise 3. Reperfusion injury 4. Compressive dressings ** Most Common Site:** Calf ** High-Risk Situations :** 1. Supracondylar elbow fractures (children) 2. Proximal/midshaft tibial fractures 3. Electrical burns 4. Arterial/venous disruption ** Clinical (5 P's) :** 1. ** Pain** (out of proportion, passive movement worsens) 2. ** Paresthesia** (decreased sensation) 3. ** Paralysis** (late) 4. ** Pallor ** 5. ** Pulselessness** (late) ** Diagnosis:** Clinical + Intracompartmental pressure - Pressure >40 mmHg â†’ Fasciotomy indicated - 30-40 mmHg: Gray area ** Treatment :** 1. Initial: Bivalve/split casts, remove constricting clothes, extremity at heart level 2. Definitive: **Fasciotomy within 4 hours** (6-8 hours maximum) --- ### Orthopedic Terminology | Term | Definition | |------|------------| | ** Supination** | Palm up | | ** Pronation** | Palm down | | ** Plantarflexion** | Foot down at ankle | | ** Dorsiflexion** | Foot up at ankle | | ** Adduction** | Movement toward body | | ** Abduction** | Movement away from body | | ** Inversion** | Foot sole faces midline | | ** Eversion** | Foot sole faces laterally | | ** Volarflexion ** | Hand flexes at wrist toward flexor tendons | | ** Wrist dorsiflexion** | Hand flexes at wrist toward extensor tendons | | ** Reduction** | Maneuver to restore proper alignment | | ** Closed reduction** | Non-surgical (casts, splints) | | ** Open reduction** | Surgical | | ** Fixation** | Stabilization with hardware (pins, plates, screws) | | ** Varus** | Apex away from midline (bowlegged) | | ** Valgus** | Apex toward midline (knock-kneed) | ** Fracture Types :** | Type | Definition | |------|------------| | ** Closed fracture** | Intact skin over fracture | | ** Open (compound) fracture** | Wound overlying fracture (high infection risk) | | ** Simple fracture** | One fracture line, two fragments | | ** Comminuted fracture** | >2 fragments | | ** Segmental fracture** | Two complete fractures with segment in between | | ** Transverse fracture** | Perpendicular to long axis | | ** Oblique fracture** | Oblique angle to long axis | | ** Spiral fracture** | Rotates along long axis (twisting injury) | | ** Longitudinal fracture** | Parallel to long axis | | ** Impacted fracture** | Compressive force, bone driven into contiguous metaphysis | | ** Pathologic fracture** | Through abnormal bone (tumor, osteoporosis) | | ** Stress fracture** | Normal bone from cyclic loading | | ** Greenstick fracture** | Incomplete, cortex on one side disrupted (children) | | ** Torus (buckle) fracture** | Cortex buckled but not disrupted (children) | | ** Avulsion fracture** | Tendon pulled from bone with bone chip | | ** Intra-articular fracture** | Through articular surface (usually requires ORIF) | ** Common Named Fractures :** | Name | Description | |------|-------------| | ** Colles ' fracture** | Distal radius fracture with dorsal displacement/angulation (FOOSH) | | ** Smith's fracture** | Reverse Colles 'â€”volar displacement/angulation | | ** Jones' fracture** | Base of 5th metatarsal diaphysis | | ** Bennett's fracture** | Base of 1st metacarpal (thumb) with CMC disruption | | ** Boxer's fracture** | Metacarpal neck (classically small finger) | | ** Nightstick fracture** | Ulnar fracture | | ** Clay shoveler's** | Spinous process of C6-C7 | | ** Hangman's fracture** | Pedicles of C2 | | ** Transcervical fracture** | Through neck of femur | | ** Tibial plateau fracture** | Intra-articular proximal tibia | | ** Monteggia fracture** | Proximal 1/3 ulna + Radial head dislocation | | ** Galeazzi fracture** | Distal radius + Distal radioulnar joint disruption | | **" Pilon" fracture** | Distal tibia | | ** Pott's fracture** | Distal fibula | --- ### Structure Injury with Fractures | Fracture/Dislocation | Nerve/Artery Injured | |----------------------|---------------------| | Anterior shoulder dislocation | Axillary nerve | | Posterior shoulder dislocation (electrical shock/status epilepticus) | Axillary artery | | Proximal Hemorrhage 2. Burns 3. Bowel obstruction 4. Crush injury 5. Pancreatitis ** Signs :** - ** Early**: Orthostatic hypotension, mild tachycardia, anxiety, diaphoresis, vasoconstriction (â†“ pulse pressure, â†‘ diastolic pressure) - ** Late**: Changed mental status, â†“ BP, marked tachycardia ** Treatment :** 1. Stop bleeding 2. Volume: IVF (isotonic LR) â†’ Blood products as needed ** Stages :** | Stage | Blood Loss | Clinical Features | |-------|------------|-------------------| | **1** | <15% (750 mL) | Mild anxiety, normal vital signs | | **2** | 15-30% (750 -1500 mL) | Normal systolic BP with â†“ pulse pressure, tachycardia, tachypnea, anxiety | | **3** | 30-40% (1500 -2000 mL) | HR >120, RR >30, â†“ systolic BP, â†“ pulse pressure, confusion | | **4** | >40% ( >2000 mL) | â†“ Systolic BP, HR >140, RR >35, â†“ pulse pressure, confused/lethargic, no urine output | --- ### Septic Shock ** Definition:** Documented infection + Hypotension ** Etiology :** - Most common: Gram-negative septicemia - Less common: Gram-positive, fungus ** Risk Factors:** Trauma, immunosuppression, corticosteroids, hematologic disease, DM ** Signs :** - ** Initial**: Vasodilation â†’ Warm skin, full pulses, normal urine output - ** Delayed**: Vasoconstriction, poor urine output, mental status changes, hypotension - Fever, hyperventilation, tachycardia ** Diagnosis :** - ** Early**: Hyperglycemia/glycosuria, respiratory alkalosis, hemoconcentration, leucopenia - ** Late**: Leukocytosis, acidosis, elevated lactic acid - Blood cultures positive in only 50% ** Complications:** Multiple organ failure, DIC, death ** Treatment :** 1. Volume (IVF) 2. Antibiotics (empiric â†’ by cultures) 3. Drainage of infection 4. Pressors PRN 5. Activated protein C ( Zygris Â®) PRN --- ### Cardiogenic Shock ** Definition:** Cardiac insufficiency (usually left ventricular failure) â†’ Inadequate tissue perfusion ** Etiology :** - MI - Papillary muscle dysfunction - Massive cardiac contusion - Cardiac tamponade - Tension pneumothorax - Cardiac valve failure ** Clinical :** - Dyspnea, Rales - Pulsus alternans (â†‘ pulse with greater filling following weak pulse) - Loud pulmonic component of S2, Gallop rhythm - Hypotension, â†“ cardiac output - Elevated CVP/wedge pressure, â†“ urine output - Pulmonary edema (CXR) ** Treatment :** - CHF: Diuretics + Afterload reduction (ACE inhibitors) Â± Pressors - Left ventricular failure (MI): Pressors + Afterload reduction --- ### Neurogenic Shock ** Definition:** Inadequate tissue perfusion from loss of sympathetic vasoconstrictive tone ** Etiology :** 1. Spinal cord injury 2. Complete transection of spinal cord 3. Partial cord injury with spinal shock 4. Spinal anesthesia ** Clinical :** - Hypotension + Bradycardia (loss of sympathetic tone) - Neurologic deficits suggesting cord injury ** Treatment:** IV fluids; Vasopressors reserved for hypotension refractory to fluids * Note: Spinal shock = Complete flaccid paralysis immediately following spinal cord injury (may or may not be associated with circulatory shock) * --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø§Ù„ØµØ¯Ù…Ø© (Ø§Ù„Ø´ÙˆÙƒ) Ù‡ÙŠ Ù†Ù‚Øµ ÙÙŠ Ø§Ù„ØªØ±ÙˆÙŠØ© Ø§Ù„Ù†Ø³ÙŠØ¬ÙŠØ©. Symptoms: Hypotension, tachycardia, tracheal deviation, neck vein distention, diminished unilateral breath sounds | Needle decompression (2nd ICS, midclavicular line) â†’ Chest tube (5th ICS, midaxillary line) | | ** Hemothorax** | Ruptured blood vessel in pleural space | Chest tube + Operation if bleeding >1500 mL or >200 mL/hour | | ** Flail chest** | Multiple rib fractures preventing normal expansion â†’ Lung contusion | Diuresis + Pain control (rib blocks) + Intubation with positive pressure | --- ### Surgery Comprehensive Summary — Dr. Alaa Al-Aqrabawi  [needs review: encoding]  --- ### (D) Disability - Damage to central or peripheral nervous system - ** Spinal cord injury types **: - Complete: Loss of all function below injury - Partial: Anterior cord syndrome, Central cord syndrome, Brown- SÃ©quard syndrome --- ### Surgery Comprehensive Summary — Dr. Alaa Al-Aqrabawi  [needs review: encoding]  --- ### Volume Resuscitation - Any patient with hypovolemic shock (hypotension, tachycardia, low consciousness, pale cold skin) should receive fluid resuscitation - **3 routes **: 1. Two large catheters in upper extremity 2. Femoral vein 3. Great saphenous vein - Start with Normal saline or Ringer lactate until O-negative or type-specific blood is available --- ### Surgery Comprehensive Summary — Dr. Alaa Al-Aqrabawi  [needs review: encoding]  --- ### Surgery Comprehensive Summary — Dr. Alaa Al-Aqrabawi  [needs review: encoding]  --- ### Head Trauma ** Basal Skull Fracture :** - ** Signs **: - Raccoon eyes (periorbital ecchymoses) â†’ Anterior fossa fracture - Battle sign (mastoid ecchymoses) â†’ Middle fossa fracture - Hemotympanum + CSF leakage - CSF rhinorrhea/otorrhea - ** Diagnosis**: CT scan - ** Treatment**: Observation (no role of surgery); NG tube contraindicated - ** Surgery indications **: - Skull depressed >1 cm - Contaminated - Persistent CSF leak ** Indications for Head CT :** 1. GCS â‰¤14 2. Foreign body penetrating skull 3. Signs of basal skull fracture 4. Vomiting â‰¥2 times 5. Age â‰¥65 6. Amnesia >30 minutes 7. Open or depressed skull fracture --- ### Thoracic Trauma | Condition | Notes | |-----------|-------| | ** Open pneumothorax** | Discussed above | | ** Tension pneumothorax** | Discussed above | | ** Hemothorax** | Discussed above | | ** Flail chest** | Discussed above | | ** Cardiac tamponade** | Blood in pericardium compresses heart. **Beck's triad**: Hypotension + Distant heart sounds + â†‘CVP. Treatment: Pericardiocentesis (urgent) â†’ OR | | ** Blunt cardiac injury** | Impact on sternum â†’ â†“Cardiac output + Arrhythmia â†’ Monitor | | ** Thoracic aortic injury** | Wide mediastinum, loss of aortic knob contour, NG tube deviation | | ** Diaphragmatic rupture** | Penetrating or blunt trauma; X-ray shows; Requires operative repair | | ** Esophageal injury** | Contrast media or endoscopy; Needs repair to prevent mediastinitis | | ** Tracheal injury** | Crepitus, difficulty speaking, respiratory distress, hemoptysis; Bronchoscopy + treatment | --- ### Abdominal Trauma | | Blunt | Penetrating | |--|-------|-------------| | ** Stable patient** | FAST â†’ CT scan | | | ** Unstable patient** | FAST â†’ Exploratory laparotomy OR DPL | Emergency laparotomy | | ** Gunshot** | | Emergency laparotomy | | ** Stab wound** | | Deep â†’ Laparotomy; Superficial â†’ Wound closure | ** Abdominal Examination :** 1. Listen, observe, palpate, auscultate 2. Rectal examination: Blood from rectal/colonic injury 3. High-riding prostate/urethral bleeding/scrotal hematoma â†’ Urethral injury â†’ NO Foley catheter â†’ Suprapubic catheter ** DPL (Diagnostic Peritoneal Lavage) :** - Used in unstable blunt trauma - Positive if: >500 WBC/cc, 10 cc blood, 100,000 RBC/cc, food particles, bile/stool, amylase >175 IU ** FAST (Focused Abdominal Sonography for Trauma) :** - Sensitive and specific; Used in place of DPL - Checks: Perihepatic fossa, perisplenic fossa, pelvis, pericardium ** Abdominal CT :** - Gold standard for stable patients - Very sensitive and specific --- ### Burns ** Degrees :** | Degree | Depth | Features | |--------|-------|----------| | **1 st ** | Limited to epidermis | No blister, red, painful, heal without scar | | **2 nd ** | Extends to dermis | Blister, red, painful, may heal with scar | | **3 rd ** | Entire dermis destroyed | No pain (nerve endings damaged), Needs grafting | ** Fluid Resuscitation :** - ** Parkland Formula**: 4 mL Ã— Body weight (kg) Ã— % TBSA (2nd + 3rd degree) - Give **half** in first 8 hours, remaining in next 16 hours - Fluid: **Ringer lactate** (preferred) ** Rule of 9s** (for TBSA) : - Head & neck: 9% - Each arm: 9% - Anterior trunk: 18% - Posterior trunk: 18% - Each leg: 18% - Perineum: 1% ** Escharotomy:** Full-thickness incision through eschar (for circumferential burns) ** Sepsis Prevention:** Excision and grafting + Topical antibiotics + Frequent dressing ** Nutrition:** High calorie + High protein diet for healing --- ### Frostbite ** Definition:** Freezing and crystallization of interstitial fluid due to freezing temperature (<0Â°C) ** Most Common Sites:** Hands, feet, ears, nose ** Pathophysiology :** 1. Initial response: Vasoconstriction 2. Intermittent vasodilation (to prevent damage) 3. When skin temp <0Â°C: Permanent vasoconstriction â†’ Interstitial fluid crystallization (at -2Â°C) 4. Tissue damage: Freezing + Rewarming (reperfusion injury) â†’ Oâ‚‚ free radicals â†’ Vasoconstriction + Platelet aggregation â†’ Thrombosis â†’ Edema â†’ Compartment pressure ** Clinical :** - Redness, cyanosis - Loss of sensation - Edema, blistering - Skin ulceration - Severe: Muscle, tendon, bone involvement ** Treatment :** 1. Rapid rewarming with warm water (40-42Â°C) 2. Warm IV fluids 3. Limb elevation (decrease edema) 4. Analgesia 5. Tetanus prophylaxis 6. ** Avoid**: Massage, antibiotics 7. ** NO debridement/amputation** â†’ Needs months for frozen tissue to return normal ** Complications :** - Infection, tetanus, gangrene - Muscle atrophy, compartment syndrome - Paresthesias , cold sensitivity - Tendon stiffness --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** ØªÙ‚ÙŠÙŠÙ… Ø§Ù„Ù…ØµØ§Ø¨ ÙŠØ¨Ø¯Ø£ Ø¨Ù€ ABCDE : Ù…Ø¬Ø±Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù‡ÙˆØ§Ø¡ Ù…Ø¹ ØªØ«Ø¨ÙŠØª Ø§Ù„Ø¹Ù…ÙˆØ¯ Ø§Ù„ÙÙ‚Ø±ÙŠØŒ Ø§Ù„ØªÙ†ÙØ³ØŒ Ø§Ù„Ø¯ÙˆØ±Ø§Ù† ÙˆØ§Ù„Ù†Ø²ÙØŒ Ø§Ù„Ø¥Ø¹Ø§Ù‚Ø© Ø§Ù„Ø¹ØµØ¨ÙŠØ©ØŒ ÙˆØ§Ù„ØªØ¹Ø±ÙŠØ¶ Ù„Ù„ÙØ­Øµ. Ù‚Ø¶Ù…Ø© Ø§Ù„ØµÙ‚ÙŠØ¹ ØªØ¹Ø§Ù„Ø¬ Ø¨Ø¥Ø¹Ø§Ø¯Ø© Ø§Ù„ØªØ³Ø®ÙŠÙ† Ø§Ù„Ø³Ø±ÙŠØ¹ Ø¨Ù…Ø§Ø¡ Ø¯Ø§ÙØ¦. --- ## ðŸ“‹ CHAPTER 27: SURGICAL NUTRITION ### Enteral Nutrition ** Definition:** Delivery of nutrients into the GIT ** Indications:** Patient with inadequate oral intake but functional accessible GIT (comatose, severe dysphagia, head/neck surgery) ** Routes :** 1. Sip feeding: Whole food by mouth 2. Tube feeding : - Nasogastric tube (NGT/Ryle's tube) - Gastrostomy (if NG not possible) - Jejunostomy (if stomach diseased) ** Formulae :** - Gastrostomy: Liquid diet, juice, milk - Jejunostomy: Partially digested or elemental formulae ** Complications :** 1. Mechanical: Malposition, displacement, blockage, erosion 2. Infective: Exogenous or endogenous contamination 3. GIT: Diarrhea, bloating, nausea, vomiting, aspiration, constipation 4. Metabolic: Electrolyte imbalance, malnutrition, drug interactions --- ### Total Parenteral Nutrition (TPN) ** Definition:** All nutritional requirements through IV route without using GIT ** Indications :** 1. GIT blocked (stricture, neoplasm, extrinsic mass) 2. GIT short (short gut syndrome) 3. GIT fistulated (enterocutaneous fistula) 4. GIT inflamed (IBD) 5. GIT cannot cope (severe trauma, hypercatabolic state) ** Routes :** 1. ** Central**: Catheter via subclavian, internal/external jugular vein 2. ** Peripheral**: PICC line or short cannula (up to 2 weeks) ** Complications :** - Nutritional/metabolic: Overfeeding, underfeeding, electrolyte imbalance - Catheter : 1. Misplacement 2. Hemothorax/pneumothorax (tip in pleural cavity) 3. Artery or brachial plexus injury 4. Venous thrombosis 5. Infection â†’ Septicemia --- ### Metabolic Response to Starvation 1. â†“ Plasma insulin 2. â†‘ Plasma glucagon 3. Hepatic glycogenolysis 4. Protein catabolism 5. Hepatic gluconeogenesis 6. Lipolysis (fat mobilization) 7. Adaptive ketogenesis 8. â†“ Resting energy expenditure ### Metabolic Response to Stress & Sepsis 1. â†‘ Counter-regulatory hormones (adrenaline, noradrenaline, cortisol, glucagon, GH) 2. â†‘ Energy requirements 3. â†‘ Nitrogen requirements 4. â†‘ Gluconeogenesis + Protein catabolism 5. Insulin resistance + Glucose intolerance 6. Fluid retention + Hypoalbuminemia --- ### Key Points - Normal BMR: 30 Kcal/kg/day - Normal daily protein: 1 g/kg/day - Protein in starvation: 1.5 g/kg/day - Protein in sepsis/burn: 2 g/kg/day --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø§Ù„ØªØºØ°ÙŠØ© Ø§Ù„Ù…Ø¹ÙˆÙŠØ©: Ø¥Ø¹Ø·Ø§Ø¡ Ø§Ù„Ø·Ø¹Ø§Ù… Ø¹Ø¨Ø± Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù‡Ø§Ø² Ø§Ù„Ù‡Ø¶Ù…ÙŠ (ÙÙ…ÙˆÙŠØ§Ù‹ Ø£Ùˆ Ø¨Ø£Ù†Ø¨ÙˆØ¨) Ù„Ù„Ù…Ø±Ø¶Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù‚Ø§Ø¯Ø±ÙŠÙ† Ø¹Ù„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù‡Ø¶Ù…. Ø§Ù„ØªØºØ°ÙŠØ© Ø§Ù„ÙˆØ±ÙŠØ¯ÙŠØ© Ø§Ù„ÙƒØ§Ù…Ù„Ø©: Ø¥Ø¹Ø·Ø§Ø¡ ÙƒÙ„ Ø§Ù„Ø§Ø­ØªÙŠØ§Ø¬Ø§Øª Ø§Ù„ØºØ°Ø§Ø¦ÙŠØ© Ø¹Ø¨Ø± Ø§Ù„ÙˆØ±ÙŠØ¯ Ø¹Ù†Ø¯Ù…Ø§ ÙŠÙƒÙˆÙ† Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù‡Ø§Ø² Ø§Ù„Ù‡Ø¶Ù…ÙŠ ØºÙŠØ± Ù‚Ø§Ø¯Ø± Ø¹Ù„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ø¹Ù…Ù„ (Ø§Ù†Ø³Ø¯Ø§Ø¯ØŒ Ù†Ø§Ø³ÙˆØ±ØŒ Ø§Ù„ØªÙ‡Ø§Ø¨ Ø´Ø¯ÙŠØ¯). Ø§Ù„Ø§Ø³ØªØ¬Ø§Ø¨Ø© Ø§Ù„Ø£ÙŠØ¶ÙŠØ© Ù„Ù„Ø¬Ùˆ Ø¹ : Ø§Ù†Ø®ÙØ§Ø¶ Ø§Ù„Ø£Ù†Ø³ÙˆÙ„ÙŠÙ†ØŒ Ø§Ø±ØªÙØ§Ø¹ Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ù„ÙˆÙƒØ§Ø¬ÙˆÙ†ØŒ ØªØ­Ù„Ù„ Ø§Ù„Ø¨Ø±ÙˆØªÙŠÙ† ÙˆØ§Ù„Ø¯Ù‡ÙˆÙ†. Ø§Ù„Ø§Ø³ØªØ¬Ø§Ø¨Ø© Ù„Ù„Ø¥Ø¬Ù‡Ø§Ø¯ ÙˆØ§Ù„Ø¥Ù†ØªØ§Ù†: Ø§Ø±ØªÙØ§Ø¹ Ù‡Ø±Ù…ÙˆÙ†Ø§Øª Ø§Ù„Ø¥Ø¬Ù‡Ø§Ø¯ØŒ Ø²ÙŠØ§Ø¯Ø© Ø§Ù„Ø§Ø­ØªÙŠØ§Ø¬Ø§Øª Ù…Ù† Ø§Ù„Ø·Ø§Ù‚Ø© ÙˆØ§Ù„Ø¨Ø±ÙˆØªÙŠÙ†ØŒ Ù…Ù‚Ø§ÙˆÙ…Ø© Ø§Ù„Ø£Ù†Ø³ÙˆÙ„ÙŠÙ†. Ø§Ù„Ø§Ø­ØªÙŠØ§Ø¬ Ø§Ù„Ø·Ø¨ÙŠØ¹ÙŠ Ù„Ù„Ø¨Ø±ÙˆØªÙŠÙ† 1 Øº/ÙƒØº/ÙŠÙˆÙ…ØŒ ÙˆÙŠØ²ÙŠØ¯ ÙÙŠ Ø§Ù„Ø­Ø±ÙˆÙ‚ ÙˆØ§Ù„Ø¥Ù†ØªØ§Ù† Ø¥Ù„Ù‰ 2 Øº/ÙƒØº/ÙŠÙˆÙ…. --- ## ðŸ“‹ CHAPTER 28: WOUND HEALING ### Types of Wound Healing | Type | Description | Scar | |------|-------------|------| | ** Primary intention** | Clean incised wounds closed immediately by suture | Minimal, neat scar | | ** Secondary intention** | Gaped wounds, wound infection | More scar, ugly scar | | ** Tertiary intention** | Left open for 5 days, then delayed primary suture | | --- ### Stages of Wound Healing **1. Inflammatory Phase (1st 4 days) :** - ** Vascular response **: - Hemostasis (5-10 min): Vasoconstriction + Clotting cascade - Vasodilation (first 72 hours): Signs of inflammation (red, hot, tender) - ** Cellular response **: - ** Macrophages** (main cell) - Neutrophils - Mast cells **2. Lag/Substrate Phase (3-5 days) :** - Formation of capillary endothelium, mucopolysaccharides, collagen fibers **3. Proliferative Phase (5-21 days) :** - Epithelial regeneration - Granulation tissue formation - Connective tissue repair - Wound contraction **4. Remodeling Phase (>1 year) :** - Maturation of scar tissue ** Order of Cells Arrival :** 1. Platelets: Few hours 2. PMNs: 0-2 days 3. Macrophages (monocytes): 3-4 days 4. Fibroblasts: 5 days --- ### Factors Affecting Wound Healing | General Factors | Local Factors | |-----------------|---------------| | Age, obesity | Type of wound | | Medical conditions (DM, CRF) | Vascularity | | Malnutrition | Irradiation and immobilization | | Medications (steroids) | Tension | | Smoking | Infection and foreign body | | | Adhesion | --- ### Complications of Wound Healing 1. Wound dehiscence 2. Stretching scar 3. Contracture 4. Surgical site infection 5. Keloid scar 6. Hypertrophic scar ** Keloid vs Hypertrophic Scar :** | Feature | Keloid | Hypertrophic | |---------|--------|--------------| | Extent | Extends beyond scar | Doesn't extend beyond margins | | Growth | Slowly growing months-years | Usually in the month | | Growing phase | Painful â†’ becomes painless | Painless | | Collagen | Irregular and thick | Parallel and thin | | Regression | Don't regress | Stable or regress with time | | Predominance | More in Blacks | All races | --- ### Types of Surgical Wounds | Type | Description | Examples | Infection Risk | |------|-------------|----------|----------------| | ** Clean** | No cavity opened, no acute inflammation, no break in aseptic technique | Thyroidectomy, Herniorrhaphy | 1% | | ** Clean-contaminated** | Cavity opened (chest, abd , pelvis), no acute inflammation | Cholecystectomy, Elective colon resection | 5% | | ** Contaminated** | Major break in aseptic technique OR Gross GI spillage OR Non-purulent infection | Inflamed appendix, Diverticulitis | 10% | | ** Dirty/infected** | Perforated bowel with peritonitis OR Intra-abdominal abscess | Perforated bowel, Intra-abdominal abscess | >30% | --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø§Ù„ØªØ¦Ø§Ù… Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ø±ÙˆØ­ Ø«Ù„Ø§Ø«Ø© Ø£Ù†ÙˆØ§Ø¹: Ø£ÙˆÙ„ÙŠ (Ø¬Ø±ÙˆØ­ Ù†Ø¸ÙŠÙØ© Ù…ØºÙ„Ù‚Ø© Ø¨Ø®ÙŠØ§Ø·Ø©ØŒ Ù†Ø¯Ø¨Ø© Ø¬Ù…ÙŠÙ„Ø©)ØŒ Ø«Ø§Ù†ÙˆÙŠ (Ø¬Ø±ÙˆØ­ Ù…ÙØªÙˆØ­Ø© Ù…ØµØ§Ø¨Ø©ØŒ Ù†Ø¯Ø¨Ø© Ù‚Ø¨ÙŠØ­Ø©)ØŒ ÙˆØ«Ø§Ù„Ø«ÙŠ (ØªØªØ±Ùƒ Ù„Ù…Ø¯Ø© 5 Ø£ÙŠØ§Ù… Ø«Ù… ØªØ®Ø§Ø·). Ø£Ù†ÙˆØ§Ø¹ Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ø±ÙˆØ­ Ø§Ù„Ø¬Ø±Ø§Ø­ÙŠØ©: Ù†Ø¸ÙŠÙ (1% Ø¹Ø¯ÙˆÙ‰)ØŒ Ù†Ø¸ÙŠÙ-Ù…Ù„ÙˆØ« (5%)ØŒ Ù…Ù„ÙˆØ« (10%)ØŒ ÙˆÙ‚Ø°Ø± (Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø± Ù…Ù† 30%). --- ## ðŸ“‹ CHAPTER 29: HEAD AND NECK ### Salivary Stones (Sialolithiasis) ** Most Common:** Submandibular gland (more viscid secretion, duct may be blocked by food) ** Clinical :** 1. Pain: Salivary colic (especially during meals) â†’ May refer to tooth, ear, or tongue 2. Swelling of affected gland ** Complications:** Sialectasis , sialadenitis, fistula ** Diagnosis:** X-ray, Sialography ** Treatment :** - Stone in duct â†’ Duct opened and stone extracted - Stone in gland : - Submandibular â†’ Submandibular sialadenectomy - Parotid â†’ Superficial conservative sialadenectomy --- ### Salivary Gland Neoplasms ** Pleomorphic Adenoma :** - Most common in parotid - Benign tumor, arises from superficial part - ** Clinical**: Painless, slowly growing swelling in side of face; Well-defined, lobulated, freely mobile, variable consistency (firm or cystic, never hard); Elevates lobule of ear; No cervical LNs or facial nerve infiltration - ** Diagnosis**: CT scan, Tc-99 scan (cold spot) - ** Treatment **: - Parotid: Conservative superficial parotidectomy (superficial) OR Total conservative parotidectomy (deep) - Submandibular: Submandibular sialadenectomy ** Warthin's Tumor (Adenolymphoma, Monomorphic Adenoma) :** - Benign tumor, arises from superficial part/lower lobe of parotid - Elderly male >50 years - ** Clinical**: Painless, slowly growing swelling - ** Diagnosis**: CT, Tc-99 (hot spot) - ** Treatment**: Conservative superficial parotidectomy or enucleation --- ### Salivary Gland Carcinoma ** Etiology :** 1. De novo 2. On top of mixed parotid tumor ** Pathology :** 1. ** Mucoepidermoid carcinoma** (most common) â†’ Duct epithelium 2. ** Adenoid cystic carcinoma ( Cylindroma )** â†’ "Swiss-cheese pattern"; Myoepithelial cells (basophils) + Epithelial cells (eosinophils) 3. ** Acinic cell carcinoma** â†’ Serous acini (only in parotid) 4. ** Adenocarcinoma** (rare): Tubular, papillary, undifferentiated 5. Carcinoma ex pleomorphic adenoma 6. Undifferentiated ** Clinical :** 1. Pain (may radiate to ear, increases with mastication) 2. Swelling: Rapidly growing, warm, mildly tender, ill-defined edge, hard, infiltrates skin/muscle/nerves 3. Enlarged hard cervical lymph nodes ** Diagnosis :** - Tc-99 (cold spot) - Biopsy - CT scan - FNAB from LN - Metastatic workup ** Treatment :** - ** Parotid**: Total radical parotidectomy + Total block dissection of neck LNs + Postoperative radiotherapy - ** Submandibular**: Commando operation - ** Inoperable**: Palliative resection + Radiotherapy --- **Ø´Ø±Ø­ Ù…Ø¨Ø³Ø·:** Ø­ØµÙˆØ§Øª Ø§Ù„ØºØ¯Ø¯ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ø¹Ø§Ø¨ÙŠØ© Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø± Ø´ÙŠÙˆØ¹Ø§Ù‹ ÙÙŠ Ø§Ù„ØºØ¯Ø© ØªØ­Øª Ø§Ù„ÙÙƒ Ø§Ù„Ø³ÙÙ„ÙŠ (Ù„Ø£Ù† Ø¥ÙØ±Ø§Ø²Ù‡Ø§ Ø£ÙƒØ«Ø± Ù„Ø²ÙˆØ¬Ø©)ØŒ ÙˆØªØ³Ø¨Ø¨ Ø£Ù„Ù…Ø§Ù‹ ÙˆØªÙˆØ±Ù…Ø§Ù‹ Ø£Ø«Ù†Ø§Ø¡ Ø§Ù„Ø£ÙƒÙ„.
